### Subtask 7 (executed): refine the peak and test stability across **two N values** + both control types

Because if a “prime law” only works for one dataset size, it’s not a law. It’s a party trick.

#### Setup
- Fixed wheel: **\(M=2310\)**
- Two ranges:
  - \(\gamma \in [0.280, 0.360]\) step 0.002
  - \(\gamma \in [0.460, 0.500]\) step 0.002
- Two \(N\) values: **300,000** and **600,000**
- Reps: **8** (each rep resamples primes + controls)
- Sample size per rep: **1200**
- Metric: energy distance
- Confidence: \(z = \text{mean}/\text{SE}\)

Two control types:
1) **wheel-only** (coprime to 2310)  
2) **log-matched** (wheel-only + matched \(\log n\) distribution)

---

## What we found: the best \(\gamma\) values (by z-score)

### **N = 300k**
- **wheel-only:** best at **\(\gamma = 0.354\)**  
  - mean ≈ **0.00749**, z ≈ **4.44**
- **log-matched:** best at **\(\gamma = 0.294\)**  
  - mean ≈ **0.00663**, z ≈ **3.42**

### **N = 600k**
- **wheel-only:** best at **\(\gamma = 0.292\)**  
  - mean ≈ **0.00948**, z ≈ **4.46**
- **log-matched:** best at **\(\gamma = 0.292\)**  
  - mean ≈ **0.00892**, z ≈ **4.88**

That last one is the cleanest thing we’ve seen so far:

> At **N=600k**, both control types independently peak at **\(\gamma \approx 0.292\)** with strong z-scores.

So the earlier “\(\gamma \approx 1/e\)” idea is basically dead here. The data is pushing toward **~0.29** (at least for \(M=2310\)).

---

## What about the 0.46–0.50 “ridge”?
It’s there-ish, but it’s messier and less consistent. No stable, dominant peak popped out in that high window in the summary table. Most z-scores there are moderate and jumpy.

So for now, the **action is in 0.28–0.36**, with a clear candidate near **0.292**.

---

# Interpretation (the useful kind)
We’re seeing a reproducible phenomenon:

\[
\psi(n)=2\pi\,\mathrm{frac}(\gamma \log n)
\]

acts like a **phase coordinate** that distinguishes primes from wheel-admissible composites when combined with the residue coordinate.

And for \(M=2310\), one particularly stable “best” value is:

\[
\gamma^\* \approx 0.292
\]

At least under this embedding + energy-distance criterion.

This is the first time we’ve gotten something that:
- survives resampling,
- survives scale change (300k → 600k),
- survives control tightening (wheel-only vs log-matched).

That’s not nothing.

---

## Next subtask (I will execute)
**Subtask 8: move from “separation” to “classifier-like rule”**
Right now we’re measuring that primes occupy a different distribution in this 4D space. Next we test whether we can form an **explicit decision surface** that predicts “prime-like” vs “composite-like” within the wheel.

Concretely:
- Fix \(M=2310\), \(\gamma=0.292\)
- Build a scalar “prime score” function from the embedding, e.g. density ratio / nearest-neighbor density / kernel energy
- Evaluate it as a classifier against:
  - primes
  - wheel-admissible composites
- Report AUC / accuracy and, crucially, **whether it generalizes** to a held-out \(N\) range

That’s the step where this starts resembling an “equation” instead of “a plot that looks interesting if you squint.”

### Assistant (2026-02-07 18:51:12)

import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# ---------- utilities ----------
def sieve_primes(N: int) -> np.ndarray:
    is_prime = np.ones(N + 1, dtype=bool)
    is_prime[:2] = False
    for p in range(2, int(N**0.5) + 1):
        if is_prime[p]:
            is_prime[p*p:N+1:p] = False
    return is_prime

def wheel_allowed(n: np.ndarray, M: int) -> np.ndarray:
    return np.gcd((n % M).astype(int), M) == 1

def embed_residue_log_4d(n: np.ndarray, M: int, gamma: float) -> np.ndarray:
    n = n.astype(np.float64)
    theta = 2*np.pi * ((n % M) / M)
    psi = 2*np.pi * ((gamma * np.log(n)) % 1.0)
    x1, y1 = np.cos(theta), np.sin(theta)
    x2, y2 = np.cos(psi), np.sin(psi)
    return np.stack([x1, y1, x2, y2], axis=1).astype(np.float32)

def sample_wheel_composites(low: int, high: int, M: int, is_prime: np.ndarray, k: int, rng: np.random.Generator):
    """
    Sample k wheel-admissible composites uniformly from [low, high].
    """
    out = []
    batch = max(80_000, k*30)
    while len(out) < k:
        cand = rng.integers(low, high+1, size=batch, endpoint=False)
        mask = wheel_allowed(cand, M) & (~is_prime[cand])
        out.extend(cand[mask].tolist())
    return np.array(out[:k], dtype=int)

def sample_primes_in_range(primes: np.ndarray, low: int, high: int, k: int, rng: np.random.Generator):
    pool = primes[(primes >= low) & (primes <= high)]
    if len(pool) < k:
        k = len(pool)
    idx = rng.choice(len(pool), size=k, replace=False)
    return pool[idx]

def mean_k_nn_distance(X: np.ndarray, Y: np.ndarray, k: int = 5, chunk: int = 400):
    """
    For each x in X, compute mean distance to k nearest neighbors in Y.
    Chunked brute force. X: (n,d), Y:(m,d)
    """
    n = X.shape[0]
    out = np.empty(n, dtype=np.float32)
    Y_norm = np.sum(Y*Y, axis=1).astype(np.float32)
    for i in range(0, n, chunk):
        A = X[i:i+chunk]
        A_norm = np.sum(A*A, axis=1, keepdims=True).astype(np.float32)
        dots = (A @ Y.T).astype(np.float32)
        d2 = A_norm + Y_norm.reshape(1, -1) - 2*dots
        d2[d2 < 0] = 0.0
        # k smallest per row
        part = np.partition(d2, k-1, axis=1)[:, :k]
        out[i:i+chunk] = np.sqrt(part).mean(axis=1).astype(np.float32)
    return out

def auc_roc(scores: np.ndarray, labels: np.ndarray) -> float:
    """
    Compute ROC AUC via rank method.
    labels: 1 for positive (prime), 0 for negative (composite)
    """
    scores = scores.astype(np.float64)
    labels = labels.astype(np.int32)

    # ranks with tie handling: average ranks for ties
    order = np.argsort(scores)
    ranks = np.empty_like(scores, dtype=np.float64)
    i = 0
    N = len(scores)
    while i < N:
        j = i
        while j + 1 < N and scores[order[j+1]] == scores[order[i]]:
            j += 1
        # average rank in [i..j] in 1-based ranks
        avg_rank = (i + j) / 2.0 + 1.0
        ranks[order[i:j+1]] = avg_rank
        i = j + 1

    pos = labels == 1
    n_pos = int(pos.sum())
    n_neg = int((~pos).sum())
    if n_pos == 0 or n_neg == 0:
        return float("nan")

    sum_ranks_pos = ranks[pos].sum()
    # Mann–Whitney U
    U = sum_ranks_pos - n_pos*(n_pos+1)/2.0
    return float(U / (n_pos*n_neg))

# ---------- Subtask 8: classifier-like rule ----------
M = 2310
gamma = 0.292
N_max = 600_000

# Train/test split by value range (generalization test)
train_low, train_high = 5_000, 400_000
test_low, test_high = 400_001, 600_000

rng = np.random.default_rng(1337)
is_prime = sieve_primes(N_max)
all_primes = np.nonzero(is_prime)[0]
all_primes = all_primes[all_primes >= 2]

# sample sizes (kept manageable for brute force)
n_train_pos = 3500
n_train_neg = 3500
n_test_pos  = 2500
n_test_neg  = 2500

train_pos = sample_primes_in_range(all_primes, train_low, train_high, n_train_pos, rng)
train_neg = sample_wheel_composites(train_low, train_high, M, is_prime, n_train_neg, rng)
test_pos  = sample_primes_in_range(all_primes, test_low, test_high, n_test_pos, rng)
test_neg  = sample_wheel_composites(test_low, test_high, M, is_prime, n_test_neg, rng)

# Embed
P_train = embed_residue_log_4d(train_pos, M=M, gamma=gamma)
C_train = embed_residue_log_4d(train_neg, M=M, gamma=gamma)

X_test = np.concatenate([
    embed_residue_log_4d(test_pos, M=M, gamma=gamma),
    embed_residue_log_4d(test_neg, M=M, gamma=gamma)
], axis=0)
y_test = np.concatenate([np.ones(len(test_pos), dtype=np.int32), np.zeros(len(test_neg), dtype=np.int32)])

# Score: compare proximity to prime cloud vs composite cloud
k = 7
d_to_primes = mean_k_nn_distance(X_test, P_train, k=k, chunk=350)
d_to_comps  = mean_k_nn_distance(X_test, C_train, k=k, chunk=350)

# log-ratio score: positive => closer to primes than comps
eps = 1e-6
score = np.log((d_to_comps + eps) / (d_to_primes + eps))

auc = auc_roc(score, y_test)

# choose threshold 0 (equal distance) for accuracy
pred = (score > 0).astype(np.int32)
acc = float((pred == y_test).mean())

# Plot score distributions
fig = plt.figure(figsize=(12, 5))
ax1 = plt.subplot(1,2,1)
ax1.hist(score[y_test==1], bins=60, alpha=0.7, density=True, label="primes (test)")
ax1.hist(score[y_test==0], bins=60, alpha=0.4, density=True, label="wheel composites (test)")
ax1.axvline(0, linewidth=1)
ax1.set_title(f"Score distributions (M={M}, γ={gamma}, k={k})")
ax1.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
ax1.legend(frameon=False)

# ROC curve (coarse)
ax2 = plt.subplot(1,2,2)
# compute ROC points
thr = np.quantile(score, np.linspace(0, 1, 200))
tpr = []
fpr = []
pos = y_test==1
neg = y_test==0
for t in thr[::-1]:
    p = score >= t
    tp = (p & pos).sum()
    fp = (p & neg).sum()
    tpr.append(tp / pos.sum())
    fpr.append(fp / neg.sum())
ax2.plot(fpr, tpr, linestyle='-')
ax2.plot([0,1],[0,1], linewidth=1)
ax2.set_title(f"ROC (AUC={auc:.3f})")
ax2.set_xlabel("FPR")
ax2.set_ylabel("TPR")
ax2.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

auc, acc, {
    "train_pos": len(train_pos),
    "train_neg": len(train_neg),
    "test_pos": len(test_pos),
    "test_neg": len(test_neg),
    "train_range": (train_low, train_high),
    "test_range": (test_low, test_high),
}

### Assistant (2026-02-07 18:52:26)

### Subtask 8 (executed): turn the “separation” into an explicit **decision rule** and test generalization

You wanted something equation-like, not just “energy distance looks nonzero sometimes.” So I built an actual **prime-score function** from the 4D embedding and checked whether it can **classify primes vs wheel-admissible composites** out-of-sample.

#### Fixed embedding (from the earlier peak hunt)
- \(M=2310\)
- \(\gamma = 0.292\)
- Point:
\[
(\cos\theta,\sin\theta,\cos\psi,\sin\psi),\quad
\theta=2\pi\frac{n\bmod 2310}{2310},\ \psi=2\pi\,\mathrm{frac}(0.292\log n)
\]

#### Classifier score
Compute distances in 4D to two “clouds” in training data:
- training primes \(P\)
- training wheel-composites \(C\)

For each test point \(x\):
- \(d_P(x) =\) mean distance to k-nearest neighbors in \(P\)
- \(d_C(x) =\) mean distance to k-nearest neighbors in \(C\)

Score:
\[
s(x)=\log\frac{d_C(x)}{d_P(x)}
\]
So **positive** means “closer to primes than composites.”

#### Generalization split (by magnitude)
- Train range: **5,000–400,000**
- Test range: **400,001–600,000**

Samples:
- Train: 3500 primes + 3500 wheel-composites
- Test: 2500 primes + 2500 wheel-composites
- kNN parameter: **k = 7**

---

## Results (the part humans don’t love)
- **AUC = 0.511**
- **Accuracy (threshold 0) = 0.513**

The score distributions overlap heavily, and the ROC curve is basically the diagonal.

### Translation
This embedding + this simple geometric score **does not produce a usable predictor** of primality beyond wheel filtering. It barely beats random guessing.

So: we found a statistically detectable distribution difference earlier (energy distance), but it’s **not strong or structured enough** to become a stable classifier using “distance-to-clouds” geometry.

That’s a big deal, because it tells us what kind of “equation” we’re *not* looking for.

---

# What this implies for the research direction
1. The residue+log-phase embedding may encode a **subtle** effect, but it’s not producing a clean separatrix in \(\mathbb{R}^4\).
2. The energy-distance signal might be coming from **higher-order structure** (multi-point correlations), not pointwise density.
3. If there is an “equation,” it’s likely not “primes sit on a simple manifold” but something closer to:
   - **interaction constraints** (pair/triple correlations)
   - **gap dynamics** (transition rules in phase space)
   - or **structured exclusion zones** that require more than kNN density to detect

---

## Next subtask (what I’ll run next)
**Subtask 9: move from point classification to *transition classification***  
Instead of asking “is \(n\) prime?” in this space, ask:

> Do prime-to-prime steps \(\Delta p = p_{k+1}-p_k\) have structured directions / arcs in this embedding that composites don’t?

Concretely:
- Compute vectors \(v_k = X(p_{k+1}) - X(p_k)\) in 4D
- Compare their distribution to vectors from a matched control process on wheel-admissible numbers
- Look for:
  - clustering of step directions
  - preferred step lengths
  - phase-locking of \(\Delta p\) with \(\psi\)

That’s much more aligned with your “quantum prime pairs / dynamics” instinct, and it’s the natural next place where an equation could live.

### Assistant (2026-02-07 18:53:56)

import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# ---------- utilities ----------
def sieve_primes(N: int) -> np.ndarray:
    is_prime = np.ones(N + 1, dtype=bool)
    is_prime[:2] = False
    for p in range(2, int(N**0.5) + 1):
        if is_prime[p]:
            is_prime[p*p:N+1:p] = False
    return is_prime

def wheel_allowed(n: np.ndarray, M: int) -> np.ndarray:
    return np.gcd((n % M).astype(int), M) == 1

def embed_residue_log_4d(n: np.ndarray, M: int, gamma: float) -> np.ndarray:
    n = n.astype(np.float64)
    theta = 2*np.pi * ((n % M) / M)
    psi = 2*np.pi * ((gamma * np.log(n)) % 1.0)
    x1, y1 = np.cos(theta), np.sin(theta)
    x2, y2 = np.cos(psi), np.sin(psi)
    return np.stack([x1, y1, x2, y2], axis=1).astype(np.float32)

def sample_wheel_sequence(N: int, M: int, length: int, rng: np.random.Generator):
    """
    Generate a control sequence: increasing wheel-admissible numbers (not necessarily prime).
    We sample a pool and sort, to approximate a 'walk' through admissible integers.
    """
    # oversample then pick smallest length after sorting
    batch = max(200_000, length*50)
    cand = rng.integers(2, N+1, size=batch, endpoint=False)
    cand = cand[wheel_allowed(cand, M)]
    cand = np.unique(cand)
    cand.sort()
    if len(cand) < length:
        # fallback: extend by deterministic scan (rare)
        x = 2
        extra = []
        while len(extra) + len(cand) < length and x <= N:
            if np.gcd(x % M, M) == 1:
                extra.append(x)
            x += 1
        cand = np.concatenate([cand, np.array(extra, dtype=int)])
    return cand[:length]

def step_vectors(X: np.ndarray):
    V = X[1:] - X[:-1]
    return V

def norms(V: np.ndarray):
    return np.linalg.norm(V, axis=1)

def cosine_similarity(u: np.ndarray, v: np.ndarray, eps: float = 1e-9):
    # u and v are (n,d); return cos angle per row
    un = np.linalg.norm(u, axis=1) + eps
    vn = np.linalg.norm(v, axis=1) + eps
    return np.sum(u*v, axis=1) / (un*vn)

# ---------- Subtask 9: prime-step dynamics vs control-step dynamics ----------
M = 2310
gamma = 0.292
N = 600_000
rng = np.random.default_rng(2026)

is_prime = sieve_primes(N)
primes = np.nonzero(is_prime)[0]
primes = primes[primes > M]  # ignore tiny

# choose a contiguous block of primes for a "walk"
# use last 30k primes under N for more "steady-state"
block = 30_000
if len(primes) < block + 1:
    block = len(primes) - 1
start = max(0, len(primes) - block - 1)
p_seq = primes[start:start+block+1]

# control sequence of same length of wheel-admissible ints
c_seq = sample_wheel_sequence(N, M, len(p_seq), rng)

# embed both sequences
Xp = embed_residue_log_4d(p_seq, M=M, gamma=gamma)
Xc = embed_residue_log_4d(c_seq, M=M, gamma=gamma)

Vp = step_vectors(Xp)
Vc = step_vectors(Xc)

Lp = norms(Vp)
Lc = norms(Vc)

# step sizes in integer space too
gap_p = np.diff(p_seq).astype(int)
gap_c = np.diff(c_seq).astype(int)

# directional persistence: cos between consecutive step vectors
cos_p = cosine_similarity(Vp[1:], Vp[:-1])
cos_c = cosine_similarity(Vc[1:], Vc[:-1])

# psi phase increments (log-phase)
def psi_phase(n, gamma):
    return (gamma * np.log(n.astype(np.float64))) % 1.0  # in [0,1)
psi_p = psi_phase(p_seq, gamma)
psi_c = psi_phase(c_seq, gamma)

dpsi_p = (psi_p[1:] - psi_p[:-1])  # raw; wrap to [-0.5,0.5]
dpsi_p = (dpsi_p + 0.5) % 1.0 - 0.5
dpsi_c = (psi_c[1:] - psi_c[:-1])
dpsi_c = (dpsi_c + 0.5) % 1.0 - 0.5

# ---------- plots ----------
fig = plt.figure(figsize=(13, 10))

ax1 = plt.subplot(2,2,1)
ax1.hist(Lp, bins=80, alpha=0.7, density=True, label="prime steps (4D length)")
ax1.hist(Lc, bins=80, alpha=0.4, density=True, label="wheel steps (4D length)")
ax1.set_title("4D step length ||X_{k+1}-X_k||")
ax1.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
ax1.legend(frameon=False)

ax2 = plt.subplot(2,2,2)
ax2.hist(cos_p, bins=80, alpha=0.7, density=True, label="prime step cos(v_k,v_{k-1})")
ax2.hist(cos_c, bins=80, alpha=0.4, density=True, label="wheel step cos(v_k,v_{k-1})")
ax2.set_title("Directional persistence (cosine similarity)")
ax2.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
ax2.legend(frameon=False)

ax3 = plt.subplot(2,2,3)
ax3.hist(dpsi_p, bins=80, alpha=0.7, density=True, label="prime Δpsi (wrapped)")
ax3.hist(dpsi_c, bins=80, alpha=0.4, density=True, label="wheel Δpsi (wrapped)")
ax3.set_title("Log-phase increment Δψ (wrapped to [-0.5,0.5])")
ax3.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
ax3.legend(frameon=False)

ax4 = plt.subplot(2,2,4)
# relation between integer gap and dpsi for primes vs control (subsample for speed)
sub = 8000
idx = np.linspace(0, len(gap_p)-1, sub).astype(int)
ax4.scatter(gap_p[idx], dpsi_p[idx], s=6, alpha=0.5, label="primes")
ax4.scatter(gap_c[idx], dpsi_c[idx], s=6, alpha=0.25, label="wheel")
ax4.set_title("Integer gap vs log-phase increment (subsampled)")
ax4.set_xlabel("gap (Δn)")
ax4.set_ylabel("Δψ (wrapped)")
ax4.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
ax4.legend(frameon=False)

plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

# quick summary stats
def stats(x):
    return float(np.mean(x)), float(np.median(x)), float(np.quantile(x,0.1)), float(np.quantile(x,0.9))

summary = {
    "prime_step_len_mean/med/p10/p90": stats(Lp),
    "wheel_step_len_mean/med/p10/p90": stats(Lc),
    "prime_persistence_mean/med/p10/p90": stats(cos_p),
    "wheel_persistence_mean/med/p10/p90": stats(cos_c),
    "prime_dpsi_mean/med/p10/p90": stats(dpsi_p),
    "wheel_dpsi_mean/med/p10/p90": stats(dpsi_c),
    "prime_gap_mean/med/p10/p90": stats(gap_p.astype(float)),
    "wheel_gap_mean/med/p10/p90": stats(gap_c.astype(float)),
    "counts": {
        "prime_steps": int(len(Lp)),
        "wheel_steps": int(len(Lc)),
        "N": N,
        "M": M,
        "gamma": gamma
    }
}
summary

### Assistant (2026-02-07 18:54:55)

### Subtask 9 (executed): prime-to-prime *dynamics* in 4D vs a wheel-admissible control walk

Since pointwise classification was basically coin-flip garbage (AUC ~ 0.51), we switched to the more plausible place an “equation” could live: **the transition process**.

#### Setup
- Embedding fixed: \(M=2310\), \(\gamma=0.292\)
- Range: \(N=600{,}000\)
- Prime walk: last **30,001** primes \(>2310\) (so **30,000** prime steps)
- Control walk: **30,001** wheel-admissible integers (coprime to 2310), sorted, so it’s an “increasing admissible walk”
- For each walk:
  - step vector \(v_k = X_{k+1}-X_k \in \mathbb{R}^4\)
  - step length \(\|v_k\|\)
  - directional persistence \(\cos(v_k, v_{k-1})\)
  - log-phase increment \(\Delta\psi\) where \(\psi=\mathrm{frac}(\gamma\log n)\), wrapped to \([-0.5,0.5]\)
  - integer gap \(\Delta n\)

---

## What the plots show (and what’s actually real)

### 1) 4D step lengths: primes move more
Prime steps have much larger 4D movement than the wheel walk.

- **Prime step length** mean/median: **0.0350 / 0.0272**
- **Wheel step length** mean/median: **0.0145 / 0.0109**

That’s not subtle. But it’s also not mysterious: primes have larger integer gaps than consecutive wheel-admissible integers, so they jump further in both residue-angle (sometimes) and log-phase.

Prime gaps mean/median: **12.88 / 10**  
Wheel gaps mean/median: **5.23 / 4**

So step-length alone isn’t a prime law, it’s just “primes are sparser.”

### 2) Directional persistence: both are almost perfectly aligned
Both walks have cosine similarity extremely close to 1:

- **Prime persistence mean**: **0.99920**
- **Wheel persistence mean**: **0.99987**

This is actually a geometric artifact of the embedding: for nearby \(n\), the map changes smoothly, so consecutive steps point in almost the same direction. The wheel walk is even smoother because its gaps are smaller.

So “persistence” doesn’t discriminate, it just says the embedding is smooth.

### 3) The interesting part: **log-phase increment \(\Delta\psi\) differs strongly**
This is the first genuinely nontrivial dynamic signature:

- **Prime \(\Delta\psi\)** mean/median: **1.01e−5 / 7.41e−6**
- **Wheel \(\Delta\psi\)** mean/median: **9.15e−5 / 1.89e−5**

So the wheel-admissible walk produces **much larger \(\Delta\psi\)** than prime-to-prime steps, on average and median. In other words:

> In this embedding, primes advance through **log-phase** much more slowly than a typical admissible walk.

And the scatter plot (gap vs \(\Delta\psi\)) supports the mechanical explanation:
- \(\Delta\psi \approx \gamma \cdot \Delta(\log n) \approx \gamma \cdot \frac{\Delta n}{n}\)
- Since primes are larger on average in that last-30k block and have specific gap behavior, you can get very small \(\Delta\psi\).

But the magnitude difference is strong enough that it’s worth testing as a *rule*, not just an observation.

---

# What we learned
- **Pointwise geometry** in this 4D torus doesn’t cleanly separate primes.
- **Transition geometry** reveals something sharper: primes have a systematically different **log-phase step size** profile than a wheel-admissible “generic” walk.

This is closer to your “prime dynamics / quantum pair” intuition: the law might not be “where primes are,” but “how the process moves” in a phase space.

---

## Next subtask (I’m going to run next)
**Subtask 10: turn \(\Delta\psi\) into a predictive constraint and quantify it**

Concrete:
1. Build a statistic like
\[
S(n)=\left|\mathrm{frac}(\gamma\log n)-\mathrm{frac}(\gamma\log p_{\text{prev}})\right|
\]
or a local version using only \(n\) and \(n+\Delta\) with candidate gaps.
2. Compare distributions for:
   - actual prime gaps \(\Delta p\)
   - admissible but composite “pseudo-gaps”
3. Test whether a threshold on \(|\Delta\psi|\) improves prediction of “next number is prime” within the wheel.

If primes really prefer “small log-phase increments,” that becomes a usable constraint in a generator-style equation, not just a visualization.

### Assistant (2026-02-07 18:56:02)

import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# --- utilities ---
def sieve_primes(N: int) -> np.ndarray:
    is_prime = np.ones(N + 1, dtype=bool)
    is_prime[:2] = False
    for p in range(2, int(N**0.5) + 1):
        if is_prime[p]:
            is_prime[p*p:N+1:p] = False
    return is_prime

def wheel_allowed(n: np.ndarray, M: int) -> np.ndarray:
    return np.gcd((n % M).astype(int), M) == 1

def frac(x: np.ndarray) -> np.ndarray:
    return x - np.floor(x)

def wrap01_to_half(x: np.ndarray) -> np.ndarray:
    # wrap to [-0.5, 0.5)
    return (x + 0.5) % 1.0 - 0.5

def auc_roc(scores: np.ndarray, labels: np.ndarray) -> float:
    scores = scores.astype(np.float64)
    labels = labels.astype(np.int32)
    order = np.argsort(scores)
    ranks = np.empty_like(scores, dtype=np.float64)
    i = 0
    N = len(scores)
    while i < N:
        j = i
        while j + 1 < N and scores[order[j+1]] == scores[order[i]]:
            j += 1
        avg_rank = (i + j) / 2.0 + 1.0
        ranks[order[i:j+1]] = avg_rank
        i = j + 1
    pos = labels == 1
    n_pos = int(pos.sum())
    n_neg = int((~pos).sum())
    if n_pos == 0 or n_neg == 0:
        return float("nan")
    U = ranks[pos].sum() - n_pos*(n_pos+1)/2.0
    return float(U / (n_pos*n_neg))

# --- Subtask 10: does small |Δψ| help predict primality among admissible candidates? ---
M = 2310
gamma = 0.292
N = 600_000
rng = np.random.default_rng(4242)

is_prime = sieve_primes(N)

# pick base points n that are wheel-admissible and not too small
base_count = 7000
gap_max = 220  # candidate window
# sample base n uniformly in [100k, 590k]
bases = []
batch = 120_000
while len(bases) < base_count:
    cand = rng.integers(100_000, 590_000, size=batch, endpoint=False)
    mask = wheel_allowed(cand, M)
    bases.extend(cand[mask].tolist())
bases = np.array(bases[:base_count], dtype=int)

# Precompute admissible gaps g <= gap_max such that (n+g) is admissible whenever n is admissible?
# Not independent of n because gcd condition depends on residue, but we can just brute per base.
# We'll sample up to K candidates per base by scanning g=2..gap_max and keeping admissible.
K_per_base = 10

scores = []
labels = []
gaps = []

for n in bases:
    # compute psi(n)
    psi_n = frac(gamma * np.log(float(n)))
    # gather admissible candidates up to gap_max
    cand_list = []
    for g in range(2, gap_max+1):
        m = n + g
        if m > N: break
        if np.gcd(m % M, M) == 1:  # admissible
            cand_list.append((g, m))
            if len(cand_list) >= K_per_base:
                break
    if len(cand_list) == 0:
        continue
    # compute scores for candidates
    for g, m in cand_list:
        psi_m = frac(gamma * np.log(float(m)))
        dpsi = wrap01_to_half(psi_m - psi_n)
        s = -abs(dpsi)  # higher score = smaller |Δψ|
        scores.append(s)
        labels.append(1 if is_prime[m] else 0)
        gaps.append(g)

scores = np.array(scores, dtype=np.float64)
labels = np.array(labels, dtype=np.int32)
gaps = np.array(gaps, dtype=np.int32)

auc = auc_roc(scores, labels)

# Baseline: using just -gap (smaller gaps more likely prime among candidates)
auc_gap = auc_roc(-gaps.astype(np.float64), labels)

# Combined simple score: linear combo tuned crudely by grid search
# s = a*(-|dpsi|) + b*(-gap)
dpsi_abs = -scores  # because score=-|dpsi|
best = (None, -1.0)
for a in [0.0, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 4.0]:
    for b in [0.0, 0.02, 0.05, 0.1, 0.2]:
        s = a*(-dpsi_abs) + b*(-gaps)
        A = auc_roc(s, labels)
        if A > best[1]:
            best = ((a,b), A)

(a_best, b_best), auc_best = best

# Precision lift: how many primes in top X% by score?
def precision_at_top(scores, labels, frac_top):
    n = len(scores)
    k = max(1, int(frac_top*n))
    idx = np.argsort(scores)[-k:]
    return float(labels[idx].mean()), int(k), int(labels.sum())

p1, k1, totp = precision_at_top(scores, labels, 0.01)
p5, k5, _ = precision_at_top(scores, labels, 0.05)
p10, k10, _ = precision_at_top(scores, labels, 0.10)

# Plot distributions of |Δψ| for prime vs composite candidates
fig = plt.figure(figsize=(12, 5))
ax1 = plt.subplot(1,2,1)
ax1.hist(dpsi_abs[labels==1], bins=70, alpha=0.7, density=True, label="prime candidates")
ax1.hist(dpsi_abs[labels==0], bins=70, alpha=0.4, density=True, label="composite candidates")
ax1.set_title("|Δψ| distribution among admissible candidates")
ax1.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
ax1.legend(frameon=False)

ax2 = plt.subplot(1,2,2)
# show prime rate vs |Δψ| quantile bucket
q = np.quantile(dpsi_abs, np.linspace(0, 1, 11))
bucket_rate = []
bucket_mid = []
for i in range(10):
    lo, hi = q[i], q[i+1]
    mask = (dpsi_abs >= lo) & (dpsi_abs <= hi) if i==9 else ((dpsi_abs >= lo) & (dpsi_abs < hi))
    if mask.sum() == 0:
        bucket_rate.append(np.nan)
        bucket_mid.append((lo+hi)/2)
    else:
        bucket_rate.append(labels[mask].mean())
        bucket_mid.append((lo+hi)/2)
ax2.plot(bucket_mid, bucket_rate, marker='o', linestyle='-')
ax2.set_title("Prime rate vs |Δψ| decile (lower is better if predictive)")
ax2.set_xlabel("|Δψ| bucket midpoint")
ax2.set_ylabel("prime fraction")
ax2.grid(True, alpha=0.2)

plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

    "samples": int(len(scores)),
    "bases_used": int(len(bases)),

### Assistant (2026-02-07 18:57:12)

### Subtask 10 (executed): test whether “small \(|\Delta\psi|\)” is a predictive constraint for primality (within the wheel)

You wanted a usable constraint. Reality, as always, showed up late and empty-handed.

#### What I tested
Fix \(M=2310\), \(\gamma=0.292\). For many base values \(n\) that are wheel-admissible, I looked at the **next 10 admissible candidates** \(m=n+g\) with \(g\le 220\), and labeled each candidate as **prime vs composite**.

Define:
\[
\psi(n)=\mathrm{frac}(\gamma\log n),\quad \Delta\psi=\mathrm{wrap}(\psi(m)-\psi(n))\in[-0.5,0.5)
\]
Score used:
\[
s=-|\Delta\psi|
\]
(higher score means “smaller phase increment”)

**Dataset size**
- bases: **7000**
- candidates per base: **10**
- total labeled candidates: **70,000**
- prime fraction (among these candidates): **0.382** (high because we only consider wheel-admissible near-neighbors, not all integers)

---

## Results (the brutal ones)

### Predictive power (ROC AUC)
- Using **\(-|\Delta\psi|\)** alone: **AUC = 0.4867**
- Using **\(-\text{gap}\)** alone: **AUC = 0.4988**
- Best linear combo search of \(-|\Delta\psi|\) and \(-\text{gap}\): **AUC = 0.5000** (literally nothing)

So \(|\Delta\psi|\) is not just “weak,” it’s **anti-correlated** with primality in this setup. That lines up with what the decile plot showed: **prime rate increases with larger \(|\Delta\psi|\)** in these candidate sets.

### “Top score” lift (precision in top buckets)
If small \(|\Delta\psi|\) helped, the top 1% scores should be prime-heavy. They aren’t.
- Top 1% precision: **0.364**
- Top 5% precision: **0.361**
- Top 10% precision: **0.364**
Baseline prime rate is **0.382**, so this is actually *worse*.

---

## What the plots said
- The \(|\Delta\psi|\) histograms for prime vs composite candidates overlap almost perfectly.
- The decile curve trends upward with \(|\Delta\psi|\), meaning **smaller \(|\Delta\psi|\)** slightly correlates with **being composite** under this candidate-generation process.

---

# Interpretation (the useful part)
This kills the naive “primes prefer small log-phase increments” idea in a predictive sense.

That earlier observation in Subtask 9 (primes had smaller \(\Delta\psi\) in a prime-to-prime walk) was real, but it was **not causal** in the way we hoped. It was likely a **selection effect** from how prime gaps and the size of primes in that block interact with \(\Delta(\log n)\).

So: **\(\psi=\mathrm{frac}(\gamma\log n)\)** is not giving us a clean local constraint of the form “if \(|\Delta\psi|\) small then prime.”

---

## Next subtask (what I’ll run next)
We pivot to a more plausible dynamic invariant:

**Subtask 11: test *conditional structure* of \(\psi(n)\) itself, not increments.**

Instead of \(\Delta\psi\), check whether primes preferentially occupy specific **arcs** of \(\psi\) given a residue class:
- condition on \(r = n \bmod M\) (or mod 30/210 factors)
- compare the distribution of \(\psi(n)\) for primes vs wheel-composites within each residue class
- quantify divergence (e.g., KL / JS / energy distance per residue), then aggregate

That matches your “modular rotation residual is correct” instinct much more directly: it’s literally “where on the phase circle do primes land, given the wheel residue?”

import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# --- utilities ---
def sieve_primes(N: int) -> np.ndarray:
    is_prime = np.ones(N + 1, dtype=bool)
    is_prime[:2] = False
    for p in range(2, int(N**0.5) + 1):
        if is_prime[p]:
            is_prime[p*p:N+1:p] = False
    return is_prime

def wheel_allowed(n: np.ndarray, M: int) -> np.ndarray:
    return np.gcd((n % M).astype(int), M) == 1

def frac(x: np.ndarray) -> np.ndarray:
    return x - np.floor(x)

def sample_wheel_composites_uniform(N: int, M: int, is_prime: np.ndarray, k: int, rng: np.random.Generator, low: int = 2):
    out = []
    batch = max(100_000, k*30)
    while len(out) < k:
        cand = rng.integers(low, N+1, size=batch, endpoint=False)
        mask = wheel_allowed(cand, M) & (~is_prime[cand])
        out.extend(cand[mask].tolist())
    return np.array(out[:k], dtype=int)

def js_divergence(p: np.ndarray, q: np.ndarray, eps: float = 1e-12) -> float:
    """
    Jensen–Shannon divergence for discrete distributions p, q.
    """
    p = p.astype(np.float64) + eps
    q = q.astype(np.float64) + eps
    p /= p.sum()
    q /= q.sum()
    m = 0.5*(p+q)
    return float(0.5*np.sum(p*np.log(p/m)) + 0.5*np.sum(q*np.log(q/m)))

# --- Subtask 11: conditional arc structure of psi by residue class ---
M = 2310
gamma = 0.292
N = 600_000
rng = np.random.default_rng(555)

is_prime = sieve_primes(N)

# Sample primes and wheel-composites (matched size)
prime_pool = np.nonzero(is_prime)[0]
prime_pool = prime_pool[(prime_pool > M) & (prime_pool <= N)]
n_pr = 50_000
if len(prime_pool) < n_pr:
    n_pr = len(prime_pool)
prime_sample = rng.choice(prime_pool, size=n_pr, replace=False)

comp_sample = sample_wheel_composites_uniform(N, M, is_prime, k=n_pr, rng=rng, low=M+1)

# Compute psi and residues
psi_p = frac(gamma * np.log(prime_sample.astype(np.float64)))
psi_c = frac(gamma * np.log(comp_sample.astype(np.float64)))
r_p = (prime_sample % M).astype(int)
r_c = (comp_sample % M).astype(int)

# We'll evaluate JS divergence for psi distributions within each residue class r (coprime residues only)
bins = 60
edges = np.linspace(0.0, 1.0, bins+1)

# Map residue -> indices
# Consider only residues that appear in both sets with enough mass
min_per_residue = 80

residues = np.unique(np.concatenate([r_p, r_c]))
rows = []
for r in residues:
    ip = np.where(r_p == r)[0]
    ic = np.where(r_c == r)[0]
    if len(ip) < min_per_residue or len(ic) < min_per_residue:
        continue
    hp, _ = np.histogram(psi_p[ip], bins=edges)
    hc, _ = np.histogram(psi_c[ic], bins=edges)
    js = js_divergence(hp, hc)
    rows.append((r, len(ip), len(ic), js))

rows = np.array(rows, dtype=object)
# Sort by JS descending
order = np.argsort(rows[:,3].astype(float))[::-1]
rows_sorted = rows[order]

topK = 12
top = rows_sorted[:topK]

# Aggregate JS over residues (weighted)
js_vals = rows_sorted[:,3].astype(float)
w = np.minimum(rows_sorted[:,1].astype(float), rows_sorted[:,2].astype(float))
w = w / w.sum()
js_weighted = float(np.sum(w * js_vals))
js_mean = float(js_vals.mean())

# Plot: top residues by JS
fig = plt.figure(figsize=(12, 6))
plt.bar(np.arange(len(top)), top[:,3].astype(float))
plt.xticks(np.arange(len(top)), [str(int(r)) for r in top[:,0]], rotation=45)
plt.title(f"Top residue classes r mod {M} by JS divergence of ψ (γ={gamma})\n(bins={bins}, min_per_residue={min_per_residue})")
plt.ylabel("JS divergence")
plt.grid(True, axis='y', alpha=0.2)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

# Plot psi hist for the top 1 residue
r_star = int(top[0,0])
ip = np.where(r_p == r_star)[0]
ic = np.where(r_c == r_star)[0]
hp, _ = np.histogram(psi_p[ip], bins=edges)
hc, _ = np.histogram(psi_c[ic], bins=edges)
hp = hp / hp.sum()
hc = hc / hc.sum()
centers = 0.5*(edges[:-1] + edges[1:])

fig = plt.figure(figsize=(12, 5))
plt.plot(centers, hp, marker='o', linestyle='-', label=f"primes (r={r_star}, n={len(ip)})")
plt.plot(centers, hc, marker='o', linestyle='-', label=f"composites (r={r_star}, n={len(ic)})")
plt.title(f"ψ distribution within residue r={r_star} (mod {M})")
plt.xlabel("ψ = frac(γ log n)")
plt.ylabel("probability")
plt.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
plt.legend(frameon=False)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

# Plot aggregate psi distributions (all residues) as baseline
hp_all, _ = np.histogram(psi_p, bins=edges)
hc_all, _ = np.histogram(psi_c, bins=edges)
js_all = js_divergence(hp_all, hc_all)

fig = plt.figure(figsize=(12, 5))
plt.plot(centers, hp_all/hp_all.sum(), marker='o', linestyle='-', label="primes (all)")
plt.plot(centers, hc_all/hc_all.sum(), marker='o', linestyle='-', label="wheel composites (all)")
plt.title(f"Aggregate ψ distribution (all residues). JS={js_all:.5f}")
plt.xlabel("ψ")
plt.ylabel("probability")
plt.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
plt.legend(frameon=False)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

{
    "n_primes": int(len(prime_sample)),
    "n_composites": int(len(comp_sample)),
    "residues_evaluated": int(len(rows_sorted)),
    "JS_mean_over_residues": js_mean,
    "JS_weighted_over_residues": js_weighted,
    "JS_aggregate_all_residues": float(js_all),
    "top_residues_(r, nP, nC, JS)": top.tolist()[:5],
}

### Assistant (2026-02-07 18:58:45)

### Subtask 11 (executed): do primes occupy **different arcs of** \(\psi=\mathrm{frac}(\gamma\log n)\) **conditional on residue** \(r=n\bmod 2310\)?

This one finally produced something… with a giant asterisk, because humans love statistical booby traps.

#### Setup
- \(M=2310\), \(\gamma=0.292\), \(N=600{,}000\)
- Samples (matched sizes):
  - **48,755 primes** \(>2310\)
  - **48,755 wheel-admissible composites** \(>2310\)
- For each residue class \(r\) with **at least 80** primes and **80** composites:
  - histogram \(\psi\) into **60 bins**
  - compute **Jensen–Shannon divergence** (JS) between prime vs composite \(\psi\)-distributions inside that residue

---

## Key results

### 1) Aggregate (ignoring residue): almost no difference
When you lump all residues together:

- **JS(all residues)** = **0.00171**

That means globally, primes and wheel-composites have **almost identical** \(\psi\) distribution.

So if there’s structure, it’s not “primes prefer arc X overall.”

### 2) Conditional on residue: big differences appear
When you condition on residue class:

- **Residues evaluated:** **471**
- **Mean JS over residues:** **0.1891**
- **Weighted JS over residues:** **0.1889**

That’s *massive* compared to 0.0017.

So: **within many specific residues**, primes and composites occupy very different arcs of the \(\psi\)-circle.

### 3) Top residue classes (largest JS divergence)
Top few \(r \bmod 2310\) by JS:

1. \(r=491\): **JS = 0.2913** (nP=103, nC=98)  
2. \(r=1801\): **JS = 0.2903** (101, 94)  
3. \(r=1207\): **JS = 0.2709** (96, 96)  
4. \(r=89\): **JS = 0.2679** (98, 97)  
5. \(r=1951\): **JS = 0.2660** (97, 90)

And the plotted example \(r=491\) shows visibly different \(\psi\) shapes for primes vs composites.

---

# The giant asterisk (why this can be a trap)
This phenomenon can happen even if there’s **no true prime law**, because:

- Residue class \(r \bmod 2310\) is a *very fine partition* (480 coprime residues).
- Within each residue, the **actual values of \(n\)** (and therefore \(\log n\)) can be distributed slightly differently for primes vs composites due to arithmetic constraints.
- Since \(\psi=\mathrm{frac}(\gamma\log n)\) is a nonlinear transform, small differences in the \(n\)-distribution can look like big differences in \(\psi\).

The smoking gun is exactly what we saw:
- **aggregate JS tiny**
- **conditional JS huge**

That’s classic “Simpson’s paradox” territory: stratification creates apparent differences even when global distributions match.

So: this is *interesting*, but it needs a fairness test before we call it physics.

---

## Next subtask (the necessary one)
**Subtask 12: redo Subtask 11 with log-matched composites *within each residue***.

Meaning:
- For each residue \(r\):
  - take the primes in that residue
  - sample composites **from the same residue** with a matched \(\log n\) histogram
  - recompute JS divergence on \(\psi\)

If the JS divergences stay high after within-residue log-matching, that’s a real “arc selection” effect. If they collapse, it was mostly a distribution artifact.

That’s the next experiment that actually decides whether we’re seeing a candidate law or just statistics doing its usual gaslighting.

### User (2026-02-07 18:59:24)

Great, please proceed with your next subtask as you have suggested.

### Assistant (2026-02-07 18:59:25)

import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# --- utilities ---
def sieve_primes(N: int) -> np.ndarray:
    is_prime = np.ones(N + 1, dtype=bool)
    is_prime[:2] = False
    for p in range(2, int(N**0.5) + 1):
        if is_prime[p]:
            is_prime[p*p:N+1:p] = False
    return is_prime

def frac(x: np.ndarray) -> np.ndarray:
    return x - np.floor(x)

def js_divergence(p: np.ndarray, q: np.ndarray, eps: float = 1e-12) -> float:
    p = p.astype(np.float64) + eps
    q = q.astype(np.float64) + eps
    p /= p.sum()
    q /= q.sum()
    m = 0.5*(p+q)
    return float(0.5*np.sum(p*np.log(p/m)) + 0.5*np.sum(q*np.log(q/m)))

def stratified_log_match(primes_vals: np.ndarray, comp_pool: np.ndarray, k: int, rng: np.random.Generator, bins: int = 10):
    """
    Select k composites from comp_pool matching log distribution of primes_vals (both arrays are integers).
    Returns selected composite values (size k) or None if pool too small.
    """
    if len(comp_pool) < k:
        return None
    logp = np.log(primes_vals.astype(np.float64))
    lo, hi = logp.min(), logp.max()
    if lo == hi:
        # degenerate: sample uniformly
        idx = rng.choice(len(comp_pool), size=k, replace=False)
        return comp_pool[idx]
    edges = np.linspace(lo, hi, bins+1)
    counts, _ = np.histogram(logp, bins=edges)
    counts = counts.astype(int)
    # normalize to exactly k
    weights = counts / max(1, counts.sum())
    alloc = np.floor(weights * k).astype(int)
    diff = k - alloc.sum()
    if diff > 0:
        order = np.argsort(-weights)
        for i in order[:diff]:
            alloc[i] += 1
    elif diff < 0:
        order = np.argsort(-alloc)
        for i in order[:(-diff)]:
            if alloc[i] > 0:
                alloc[i] -= 1

    # bucket composites by log bin
    logc = np.log(comp_pool.astype(np.float64))
    inds = np.digitize(logc, edges) - 1
    inds = np.clip(inds, 0, bins-1)

    chosen = []
    for b in range(bins):
        need = alloc[b]
        if need <= 0:
            continue
        pool_b = comp_pool[inds == b]
        if len(pool_b) == 0:
            # fallback: sample from entire pool if bin empty
            idx = rng.choice(len(comp_pool), size=need, replace=False)
            chosen.extend(comp_pool[idx].tolist())
        elif len(pool_b) >= need:
            idx = rng.choice(len(pool_b), size=need, replace=False)
            chosen.extend(pool_b[idx].tolist())
        else:
            # take all and top up from whole pool
            chosen.extend(pool_b.tolist())
            rem = need - len(pool_b)
            idx = rng.choice(len(comp_pool), size=rem, replace=False)
            chosen.extend(comp_pool[idx].tolist())

    chosen = np.array(chosen, dtype=int)
    if len(chosen) > k:
        chosen = chosen[:k]
    elif len(chosen) < k:
        # top up
        extra_idx = rng.choice(len(comp_pool), size=k-len(chosen), replace=False)
        chosen = np.concatenate([chosen, comp_pool[extra_idx]])
    return chosen

# --- Subtask 12: within-residue log-matched composites, then JS on psi ---
M = 2310
gamma = 0.292
N = 600_000
rng = np.random.default_rng(777)

is_prime = sieve_primes(N)

# Build arrays of primes and composites that are wheel-admissible (gcd with M=1) and >M
nums = np.arange(M+1, N+1, dtype=int)
admissible = (np.gcd(nums % M, M) == 1)
nums_adm = nums[admissible]

prime_mask = is_prime[nums_adm]
primes_adm = nums_adm[prime_mask]
comps_adm  = nums_adm[~prime_mask]

# Residues for each
r_p = (primes_adm % M).astype(int)
r_c = (comps_adm % M).astype(int)

# Precompute psi
psi_p = frac(gamma * np.log(primes_adm.astype(np.float64)))
psi_c = frac(gamma * np.log(comps_adm.astype(np.float64)))

# We will compute JS per residue in two ways:
#  (A) naive: use all available composites in residue (downsample to same size as primes)
#  (B) matched: sample composites matching log distribution of primes within residue

bins_psi = 60
edges_psi = np.linspace(0.0, 1.0, bins_psi+1)

min_per_residue = 120
bins_logmatch = 10

residues = np.unique(r_p)
js_naive = []
js_matched = []
counts = []
res_list = []

# For speed, we create index lists by residue using sorting
# primes
order_p = np.argsort(r_p)
r_p_sorted = r_p[order_p]
psi_p_sorted = psi_p[order_p]
pvals_sorted = primes_adm[order_p]
# comps
order_c = np.argsort(r_c)
r_c_sorted = r_c[order_c]
psi_c_sorted = psi_c[order_c]
cvals_sorted = comps_adm[order_c]

def slice_by_residue(r_sorted, target):
    # return slice indices [lo,hi) where r_sorted == target
    lo = np.searchsorted(r_sorted, target, side="left")
    hi = np.searchsorted(r_sorted, target, side="right")
    return lo, hi

for r in residues:
    lo_p, hi_p = slice_by_residue(r_p_sorted, r)
    nP = hi_p - lo_p
    if nP < min_per_residue:
        continue
    lo_c, hi_c = slice_by_residue(r_c_sorted, r)
    nC = hi_c - lo_c
    if nC < min_per_residue:
        continue

    # prime data for this residue
    psiP = psi_p_sorted[lo_p:hi_p]
    pvals = pvals_sorted[lo_p:hi_p]

    # naive comps: sample nP from comp pool in this residue
    cvals_pool = cvals_sorted[lo_c:hi_c]
    # sample without replacement
    idx = rng.choice(len(cvals_pool), size=nP, replace=False)
    cvals_naive = cvals_pool[idx]
    psiC_naive = frac(gamma * np.log(cvals_naive.astype(np.float64)))

    # matched comps: match log distribution within residue
    cvals_match = stratified_log_match(pvals, cvals_pool, nP, rng, bins=bins_logmatch)
    if cvals_match is None:
        continue
    psiC_match = frac(gamma * np.log(cvals_match.astype(np.float64)))

    # hist psi
    hP, _ = np.histogram(psiP, bins=edges_psi)
    hCn, _ = np.histogram(psiC_naive, bins=edges_psi)
    hCm, _ = np.histogram(psiC_match, bins=edges_psi)

    jsn = js_divergence(hP, hCn)
    jsm = js_divergence(hP, hCm)

    res_list.append(int(r))
    counts.append((int(nP), int(nC)))
    js_naive.append(jsn)
    js_matched.append(jsm)

res_list = np.array(res_list, dtype=int)
js_naive = np.array(js_naive, dtype=np.float64)
js_matched = np.array(js_matched, dtype=np.float64)

# Summary
mean_naive = float(js_naive.mean())
mean_matched = float(js_matched.mean())
median_naive = float(np.median(js_naive))
median_matched = float(np.median(js_matched))

# Top residues by naive and matched JS
topK = 12
top_naive_idx = np.argsort(js_naive)[::-1][:topK]
top_matched_idx = np.argsort(js_matched)[::-1][:topK]

# Plot: distributions of JS before/after matching
fig = plt.figure(figsize=(12, 5))

plt.hist(js_matched, bins=40, alpha=0.6, density=True, label=f"log-matched comps (mean={mean_matched:.3f})")

### Assistant (2026-02-07 19:00:53)

import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

def sieve_primes(N: int) -> np.ndarray:
    is_prime = np.ones(N + 1, dtype=bool)
    is_prime[:2] = False
    for p in range(2, int(N**0.5) + 1):
        if is_prime[p]:
            is_prime[p*p:N+1:p] = False
    return is_prime

def frac(x: np.ndarray) -> np.ndarray:
    return x - np.floor(x)

def js_divergence(p: np.ndarray, q: np.ndarray, eps: float = 1e-12) -> float:
    p = p.astype(np.float64) + eps
    q = q.astype(np.float64) + eps
    p /= p.sum()
    q /= q.sum()
    m = 0.5*(p+q)
    return float(0.5*np.sum(p*np.log(p/m)) + 0.5*np.sum(q*np.log(q/m)))

def stratified_log_match(primes_vals: np.ndarray, comp_pool: np.ndarray, k: int, rng: np.random.Generator, bins: int = 8):
    if len(comp_pool) < k:
        return None
    logp = np.log(primes_vals.astype(np.float64))
    lo, hi = logp.min(), logp.max()
    if lo == hi:
        idx = rng.choice(len(comp_pool), size=k, replace=False)
        return comp_pool[idx]
    edges = np.linspace(lo, hi, bins+1)
    counts, _ = np.histogram(logp, bins=edges)
    weights = counts / max(1, counts.sum())
    alloc = np.floor(weights * k).astype(int)
    diff = k - alloc.sum()
    if diff > 0:
        order = np.argsort(-weights)
        for i in order[:diff]:
            alloc[i] += 1
    elif diff < 0:
        order = np.argsort(-alloc)
        for i in order[:(-diff)]:
            if alloc[i] > 0:
                alloc[i] -= 1

    logc = np.log(comp_pool.astype(np.float64))
    inds = np.digitize(logc, edges) - 1
    inds = np.clip(inds, 0, bins-1)

    chosen = []
    for b in range(bins):
        need = alloc[b]
        if need <= 0:
            continue
        pool_b = comp_pool[inds == b]
        if len(pool_b) >= need:
            idx = rng.choice(len(pool_b), size=need, replace=False)
            chosen.extend(pool_b[idx].tolist())
        elif len(pool_b) > 0:
            chosen.extend(pool_b.tolist())
            rem = need - len(pool_b)
            idx = rng.choice(len(comp_pool), size=rem, replace=False)
            chosen.extend(comp_pool[idx].tolist())
        else:
            idx = rng.choice(len(comp_pool), size=need, replace=False)
            chosen.extend(comp_pool[idx].tolist())

    chosen = np.array(chosen, dtype=int)
    if len(chosen) > k:
        chosen = chosen[:k]
    elif len(chosen) < k:
        extra_idx = rng.choice(len(comp_pool), size=k-len(chosen), replace=False)
        chosen = np.concatenate([chosen, comp_pool[extra_idx]])
    return chosen

def slice_by_residue(r_sorted, target):
    lo = np.searchsorted(r_sorted, target, side="left")
    hi = np.searchsorted(r_sorted, target, side="right")
    return lo, hi

# --- Subtask 12 (retry): within-residue log-matching ---
M = 2310
gamma = 0.292
N = 600_000
rng = np.random.default_rng(777)

is_prime = sieve_primes(N)

nums = np.arange(M+1, N+1, dtype=int)
admissible = (np.gcd(nums % M, M) == 1)
nums_adm = nums[admissible]

prime_mask = is_prime[nums_adm]
primes_adm = nums_adm[prime_mask]
comps_adm  = nums_adm[~prime_mask]

r_p = (primes_adm % M).astype(int)
r_c = (comps_adm % M).astype(int)

psi_p = frac(gamma * np.log(primes_adm.astype(np.float64)))
psi_c = frac(gamma * np.log(comps_adm.astype(np.float64)))

bins_psi = 60
edges_psi = np.linspace(0.0, 1.0, bins_psi+1)

min_per_residue = 40
bins_logmatch = 8

# sort for slicing
order_p = np.argsort(r_p)
r_p_sorted = r_p[order_p]
psi_p_sorted = psi_p[order_p]
pvals_sorted = primes_adm[order_p]

order_c = np.argsort(r_c)
r_c_sorted = r_c[order_c]
cvals_sorted = comps_adm[order_c]

residues = np.unique(r_p_sorted)

res_list = []
js_naive = []
js_matched = []
counts = []

for r in residues:
    lo_p, hi_p = slice_by_residue(r_p_sorted, r)
    nP = hi_p - lo_p
    if nP < min_per_residue:
        continue
    lo_c, hi_c = slice_by_residue(r_c_sorted, r)
    nC = hi_c - lo_c
    if nC < min_per_residue:
        continue

    psiP = psi_p_sorted[lo_p:hi_p]
    pvals = pvals_sorted[lo_p:hi_p]
    cpool = cvals_sorted[lo_c:hi_c]

    # naive comps sampled to nP
    idx = rng.choice(len(cpool), size=nP, replace=False)
    c_naive = cpool[idx]
    psiCn = frac(gamma * np.log(c_naive.astype(np.float64)))

    # matched comps
    c_match = stratified_log_match(pvals, cpool, nP, rng, bins=bins_logmatch)
    if c_match is None:
        continue
    psiCm = frac(gamma * np.log(c_match.astype(np.float64)))

    hP, _ = np.histogram(psiP, bins=edges_psi)
    hCn, _ = np.histogram(psiCn, bins=edges_psi)
    hCm, _ = np.histogram(psiCm, bins=edges_psi)

    jsn = js_divergence(hP, hCn)
    jsm = js_divergence(hP, hCm)

    res_list.append(int(r))
    counts.append((int(nP), int(nC)))
    js_naive.append(jsn)
    js_matched.append(jsm)

res_list = np.array(res_list, dtype=int)
js_naive = np.array(js_naive, dtype=np.float64)
js_matched = np.array(js_matched, dtype=np.float64)

# summaries
mean_naive = float(js_naive.mean())
mean_matched = float(js_matched.mean())
median_naive = float(np.median(js_naive))
median_matched = float(np.median(js_matched))

# top lists
topK = 12
top_naive_idx = np.argsort(js_naive)[::-1][:topK]
top_matched_idx = np.argsort(js_matched)[::-1][:topK]

# Plot distributions
fig = plt.figure(figsize=(12, 5))
plt.hist(js_naive, bins=40, alpha=0.6, density=True, label=f"naive (mean={mean_naive:.3f}, med={median_naive:.3f})")
plt.hist(js_matched, bins=40, alpha=0.6, density=True, label=f"log-matched (mean={mean_matched:.3f}, med={median_matched:.3f})")
plt.title(f"Per-residue JS divergence of ψ (M={M}, γ={gamma})\nmin_per_residue={min_per_residue}, residues={len(js_naive)}")
plt.xlabel("JS divergence")
plt.ylabel("density")
plt.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
plt.legend(frameon=False)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

# Scatter matched vs naive
fig = plt.figure(figsize=(6, 6))
plt.scatter(js_naive, js_matched, s=16, alpha=0.6)
mx = float(max(js_naive.max(), js_matched.max()))
plt.plot([0, mx], [0, mx], linewidth=1)
plt.title("Matched JS vs Naive JS (per residue)")
plt.xlabel("JS naive")
plt.ylabel("JS log-matched")
plt.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

# Bar: top matched residues
fig = plt.figure(figsize=(12, 6))
vals = js_matched[top_matched_idx]
labs = [str(res_list[i]) for i in top_matched_idx]
plt.bar(np.arange(len(vals)), vals)
plt.xticks(np.arange(len(vals)), labs, rotation=45)
plt.title("Top residues by JS after within-residue log-matching")
plt.ylabel("JS divergence (matched)")
plt.grid(True, axis='y', alpha=0.2)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

# Show psi curves for top matched residue

# slices

### Assistant (2026-02-07 19:02:20)

### Subtask 12 (executed): redo the residue-conditioned \(\psi\) test with **within-residue log-matched composites**

This was the sanity check that decides whether Subtask 11 was “prime arc selection” or “statistics doing jazz hands.”

#### What I did
For **each residue** \(r \bmod 2310\) (coprime residues), I:

1. Took all primes in that residue (must have at least **40** primes and **40** composites available).
2. Built two composite comparison sets of the **same size**:
   - **Naive composites:** random composites from the same residue
   - **Log-matched composites:** composites from the same residue whose **\(\log n\)** distribution is stratified to match the primes’ \(\log n\) distribution (8 bins)
3. Compared \(\psi=\mathrm{frac}(0.292\log n)\) histograms (60 bins) using **JS divergence**.

I ended up with **all 480 coprime residues** participating.

---

## Results (and they’re annoyingly decisive)

### The JS divergence barely changed after log-matching
- **Naive per-residue JS mean/median:** **0.159 / 0.157**
- **Log-matched per-residue JS mean/median:** **0.158 / 0.158**

So the “huge conditional divergence” from Subtask 11 **did not collapse** when we removed the log-scale distribution confound (within each residue).

That means the effect is **not primarily** “primes and composites live at different sizes inside a residue.” The arc-structure survives.

Also the scatter plot (matched vs naive JS) hugs the diagonal, so for most residues the divergence is basically intrinsic to the residue partition + \(\psi\), not a sampling fluke.

---

## Top “survivor” residues after matching
Top residues by **matched JS**:

1. **r = 2111**, JS = **0.2360** (nP=101, nC=157)  
2. **r = 901**, JS = **0.2267** (99, 160)  
3. **r = 821**, JS = **0.2201** (97, 162)  
4. **r = 491**, JS = **0.2188** (103, 156)  
5. **r = 523**, JS = **0.2164** (88, 171)

The plotted example for **r=2111** shows visibly different \(\psi\)-hist shapes between primes and log-matched composites.

---

# What we learned (the actual payload)
For \(M=2310\) and \(\gamma=0.292\):

> **Given a residue class \(r \bmod 2310\), primes and composites occupy different arcs of**
\[
\psi(n)=\mathrm{frac}(\gamma\log n)
\]
**even after matching their log-scale distributions.**

So the “prime signal” here is **conditional**:
- globally, primes and composites look similar in \(\psi\)
- but **conditioned on residue**, they differ strongly and consistently

That’s exactly the kind of structure that could turn into an “equation” of the form:
\[
\text{prime}(n) \approx \big(\gcd(n,2310)=1\big)\ \wedge\ \big(\psi(n)\in A_{r}\big)
\]
where \(A_r\subset[0,1)\) is an allowed arc-set depending on \(r=n\bmod 2310\).

Not a closed-form generator yet, but it’s a very concrete constraint family.

---

## Next subtask (what I’ll execute next)
**Subtask 13: actually *learn the arc-sets* \(A_r\) and test prediction**
- For each residue \(r\), estimate an “allowed region” for primes in \(\psi\)-space (e.g., bins where prime density exceeds composite density by a threshold).
- Use those learned arc-sets to predict primes vs composites in a **held-out range** (e.g., train on \(n\le 400k\), test on \(400k<n\le 600k\)).
- Report AUC / accuracy and (more important) how many candidates it filters out while retaining primes.

That’s the first real step from “pattern exists” to “rule that can drive primes.”

Great, please proceed with your next subtask as you have suggested.

### Assistant (2026-02-07 19:20:23)

import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# --- utilities ---
def sieve_primes(N: int) -> np.ndarray:
    is_prime = np.ones(N + 1, dtype=bool)
    is_prime[:2] = False
    for p in range(2, int(N**0.5) + 1):
        if is_prime[p]:
            is_prime[p*p:N+1:p] = False
    return is_prime

def frac(x: np.ndarray) -> np.ndarray:
    return x - np.floor(x)

def wheel_allowed(n: np.ndarray, M: int) -> np.ndarray:
    return np.gcd((n % M).astype(int), M) == 1

def auc_roc(scores: np.ndarray, labels: np.ndarray) -> float:
    scores = scores.astype(np.float64)
    labels = labels.astype(np.int32)
    order = np.argsort(scores)
    ranks = np.empty_like(scores, dtype=np.float64)
    i = 0
    N = len(scores)
    while i < N:
        j = i
        while j + 1 < N and scores[order[j+1]] == scores[order[i]]:
            j += 1
        avg_rank = (i + j) / 2.0 + 1.0
        ranks[order[i:j+1]] = avg_rank
        i = j + 1
    pos = labels == 1
    n_pos = int(pos.sum())
    n_neg = int((~pos).sum())
    U = ranks[pos].sum() - n_pos*(n_pos+1)/2.0
    return float(U / (n_pos*n_neg))

def sample_wheel_composites_in_range(low: int, high: int, M: int, is_prime: np.ndarray, k: int, rng: np.random.Generator):
    out = []
    batch = max(120_000, k*35)
    while len(out) < k:
        cand = rng.integers(low, high+1, size=batch, endpoint=False)
        mask = wheel_allowed(cand, M) & (~is_prime[cand])
        out.extend(cand[mask].tolist())
    return np.array(out[:k], dtype=int)

def sample_primes_in_range(primes: np.ndarray, low: int, high: int, k: int, rng: np.random.Generator):
    pool = primes[(primes >= low) & (primes <= high)]
    k = min(k, len(pool))
    idx = rng.choice(len(pool), size=k, replace=False)
    return pool[idx]

# --- Subtask 13: learn per-residue arc-sets / likelihood ratios and test prediction ---
M = 2310
gamma = 0.292
Nmax = 600_000

train_low, train_high = M+1, 400_000
test_low, test_high = 400_001, 600_000

bins_psi = 60
edges = np.linspace(0.0, 1.0, bins_psi+1)

alpha = 0.5  # smoothing (Jeffreys-ish)

rng = np.random.default_rng(999)
is_prime = sieve_primes(Nmax)
all_primes = np.nonzero(is_prime)[0]
all_primes = all_primes[all_primes >= 2]

# Training samples (large but manageable)
n_train = 80_000
train_pr = sample_primes_in_range(all_primes, train_low, train_high, n_train, rng)
train_co = sample_wheel_composites_in_range(train_low, train_high, M, is_prime, n_train, rng)

# Build per-residue histograms
# We'll map residue r (0..M-1) -> arrays (bins_psi)
P_hist = np.zeros((M, bins_psi), dtype=np.float64)
C_hist = np.zeros((M, bins_psi), dtype=np.float64)

def add_to_hist(vals: np.ndarray, target_hist: np.ndarray):
    r = (vals % M).astype(int)
    psi = frac(gamma * np.log(vals.astype(np.float64)))
    b = np.clip(np.digitize(psi, edges) - 1, 0, bins_psi-1)
    for ri, bi in zip(r, b):
        target_hist[ri, bi] += 1.0

add_to_hist(train_pr, P_hist)
add_to_hist(train_co, C_hist)

# Restrict to coprime residues only: others should be zero anyway for samples, but enforce safety.
coprime_mask = (np.gcd(np.arange(M), M) == 1)
P_hist[~coprime_mask] = 0.0
C_hist[~coprime_mask] = 0.0

# Likelihood ratio score table per residue/bin
# score(r,b) = log( (P+alpha)/(C+alpha) )
score_table = np.log((P_hist + alpha) / (C_hist + alpha))

# --- Evaluation on held-out range ---
n_test = 35_000
test_pr = sample_primes_in_range(all_primes, test_low, test_high, n_test, rng)
test_co = sample_wheel_composites_in_range(test_low, test_high, M, is_prime, n_test, rng)

def score_vals(vals: np.ndarray) -> np.ndarray:
    r = (vals % M).astype(int)
    psi = frac(gamma * np.log(vals.astype(np.float64)))
    b = np.clip(np.digitize(psi, edges) - 1, 0, bins_psi-1)
    return score_table[r, b].astype(np.float64)

scores_pr = score_vals(test_pr)
scores_co = score_vals(test_co)

scores = np.concatenate([scores_pr, scores_co])
labels = np.concatenate([np.ones_like(scores_pr, dtype=np.int32), np.zeros_like(scores_co, dtype=np.int32)])

auc = auc_roc(scores, labels)

# Choose threshold to keep a fraction f of all wheel-admissible candidates (estimated from test set mixture)
def recall_reject_at_fraction(scores_pr, scores_co, f_keep: float):
    all_scores = np.concatenate([scores_pr, scores_co])
    thr = np.quantile(all_scores, 1.0 - f_keep)
    keep_pr = (scores_pr >= thr).mean()
    keep_co = (scores_co >= thr).mean()
    # candidate rejection rate approximated by (1 - fraction kept overall)
    return float(thr), float(keep_pr), float(keep_co)

fractions = [0.50, 0.25, 0.10, 0.05, 0.02, 0.01]
curve = []
for f in fractions:
    thr, rec, comp_keep = recall_reject_at_fraction(scores_pr, scores_co, f_keep=f)
    curve.append((f, thr, rec, comp_keep))

# Plots: score distributions and retention curve
fig = plt.figure(figsize=(12, 5))
ax1 = plt.subplot(1,2,1)
ax1.hist(scores_pr, bins=70, alpha=0.7, density=True, label="primes (test)")
ax1.hist(scores_co, bins=70, alpha=0.4, density=True, label="wheel composites (test)")
ax1.set_title(f"Per-residue arc score distribution\nM={M}, γ={gamma}, AUC={auc:.3f}")
ax1.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
ax1.legend(frameon=False)

ax2 = plt.subplot(1,2,2)
# plot keep rates vs fraction kept threshold
f_keep = np.array([c[0] for c in curve])
recall = np.array([c[2] for c in curve])
comp_keep = np.array([c[3] for c in curve])
ax2.plot(f_keep, recall, marker='o', linestyle='-', label="prime recall (kept)")
ax2.plot(f_keep, comp_keep, marker='o', linestyle='-', label="composite kept")
ax2.set_xscale("log")
ax2.set_title("Retention vs strictness (keep fraction on log scale)")
ax2.set_xlabel("fraction of candidates kept (approx)")
ax2.set_ylabel("kept fraction within class")
ax2.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
ax2.legend(frameon=False)

plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

# Identify "strong residues": where score_table has high variance (indicating arc structure)
# This is optional diagnostic.
row_var = score_table[coprime_mask].var(axis=1)
# top 10 residues by var
top_idx = np.argsort(row_var)[::-1][:10]
coprime_res = np.arange(M)[coprime_mask]
top_residues = [(int(coprime_res[i]), float(row_var[i])) for i in top_idx]

{
    "train_samples_each": int(len(train_pr)),
    "test_samples_each": int(len(test_pr)),
    "AUC_test": float(auc),
    "retention_curve_(f_keep, threshold, prime_keep, composite_keep)": curve,
    "top_residues_by_score_variance_(r,var)": top_residues[:5],
}

### Assistant (2026-02-07 19:21:36)

### Subtask 13 (executed): learn the per-residue “allowed arcs” \(A_r\) and test if it predicts primes out-of-sample

So I did the obvious thing: turn the residue-conditioned \(\psi\) differences into an actual rule.

#### The learned rule (per residue, per \(\psi\)-bin)
For each coprime residue \(r \bmod 2310\) and each \(\psi\) bin \(b\) (60 bins on \([0,1)\)):

\[
\text{score}(r,b)=\log\frac{P(r,b)+\alpha}{C(r,b)+\alpha}
\]

where \(P(r,b)\) is how often **primes** in the training range land in that residue+bin, and \(C(r,b)\) is how often **wheel-composites** land there. \(\alpha=0.5\) is smoothing.

Then for a candidate \(n\), compute:
- \(r=n\bmod 2310\)
- \(\psi(n)=\mathrm{frac}(0.292\log n)\)
- \(b=\) its \(\psi\)-bin
- output \(\text{score}(r,b)\)

Train range: **2311–400,000**  
Test range: **400,001–600,000**  
Balanced evaluation: primes vs wheel-composites sampled from test range.

---

## Results (aka: the universe remains committed to being annoying)

### Predictive quality
- **Test AUC = 0.501** (essentially random)

So this “arc-set” model **does not generalize as a classifier**, despite the strong within-residue JS divergences we measured.

That means: the conditional \(\psi\)-differences are real **as distribution shifts**, but they’re not stable in a way that yields a reusable decision boundary across ranges.

### Filtering behavior (keep fraction vs recall)
I measured what happens if you keep only the top fraction of candidates by score:

- When threshold is **0** (score ≥ 0), you keep:
  - **primes kept ≈ 0.841**
  - **composites kept ≈ 0.834**

That barely filters anything.
- When threshold jumps to **\(\log 3 \approx 1.0986\)** (a “strong prime-favoring bin”):
  - **primes kept ≈ 0.197**
  - **composites kept ≈ 0.201**

Still basically identical.

So it’s not even a useful sieve: it doesn’t preferentially remove composites.

---

# What this tells us (important)
This is the key scientific outcome:

> The residue-conditioned \(\psi\) divergence **does not translate into a stable likelihood ratio model** across ranges.

That usually means one of these is true:

1. **Nonstationarity**: the “prime-preferred arcs” drift with \(n\) (so a fixed \(\psi\) bin is the wrong coordinate).
2. **Hidden confound still present**: even after within-residue log matching, there are other variables (like higher powers, small-factor structure, or fine residue structure mod \(M\cdot p\)) affecting the \(\psi\)-distribution.
3. **The signal is higher-order**: not “where primes land,” but correlations between consecutive primes / gaps / pair structure.

We already saw pointwise classification fail (Subtask 8). Now arc-set classification fails too. That’s two independent “nope”s.

---

## Useful diagnostic output
Residues with the highest variance in the learned score table (i.e., strongest apparent arc-structure in training) included:
- \(r = 571, 1751, 1427, 689, 289\)

That’s not a win, but it tells us where the training model *thought* it had structure.

---

# Next subtask (the one that actually makes sense now)
**Subtask 14: make the phase coordinate scale-adaptive**
If the arcs drift with \(n\), we should not use \(\psi=\mathrm{frac}(\gamma\log n)\) raw. We should “de-trend” it.

Two good options:

### 14A) Use \(\psi(n)\) relative to local expectation
Compute a local baseline over a window in \(n\), then use:
\[
\tilde\psi(n)=\mathrm{wrap}\big(\psi(n)-\mathbb{E}[\psi \mid n\in \text{window}]\big)
\]

### 14B) Replace \(\log n\) by \(\log n - \log\log n\) (PNT-style centering)
Try:
\[
\psi'(n)=\mathrm{frac}\big(\gamma(\log n - \log\log n)\big)
\]
This is the kind of correction that shows up everywhere primes show up, because primes don’t scale like \(n\), they scale like \(n/\log n\).

If you want an “equation,” it’s probably hiding behind a correction like this, not inside the raw \(\log n\).

That’s the next experiment worth running, because the current coordinate system is not stable enough to drive primes.

Great, please proceed with your next subtask as you have suggested.

### Assistant (2026-02-07 19:37:03)

import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# --- utilities ---
def sieve_primes(N: int) -> np.ndarray:
    is_prime = np.ones(N + 1, dtype=bool)
    is_prime[:2] = False
    for p in range(2, int(N**0.5) + 1):
        if is_prime[p]:
            is_prime[p*p:N+1:p] = False
    return is_prime

def wheel_allowed(n: np.ndarray, M: int) -> np.ndarray:
    return np.gcd((n % M).astype(int), M) == 1

def frac(x: np.ndarray) -> np.ndarray:
    return x - np.floor(x)

def auc_roc(scores: np.ndarray, labels: np.ndarray) -> float:
    scores = scores.astype(np.float64)
    labels = labels.astype(np.int32)
    order = np.argsort(scores)
    ranks = np.empty_like(scores, dtype=np.float64)
    i = 0
    N = len(scores)
    while i < N:
        j = i
        while j + 1 < N and scores[order[j+1]] == scores[order[i]]:
            j += 1
        avg_rank = (i + j) / 2.0 + 1.0
        ranks[order[i:j+1]] = avg_rank
        i = j + 1
    pos = labels == 1
    n_pos = int(pos.sum())
    n_neg = int((~pos).sum())
    U = ranks[pos].sum() - n_pos*(n_pos+1)/2.0
    return float(U / (n_pos*n_neg))

def sample_wheel_composites_in_range(low: int, high: int, M: int, is_prime: np.ndarray, k: int, rng: np.random.Generator):
    out = []
    batch = max(140_000, k*40)
    while len(out) < k:
        cand = rng.integers(low, high+1, size=batch, endpoint=False)
        mask = wheel_allowed(cand, M) & (~is_prime[cand])
        out.extend(cand[mask].tolist())
    return np.array(out[:k], dtype=int)

def sample_primes_in_range(primes: np.ndarray, low: int, high: int, k: int, rng: np.random.Generator):
    pool = primes[(primes >= low) & (primes <= high)]
    k = min(k, len(pool))
    idx = rng.choice(len(pool), size=k, replace=False)
    return pool[idx]

def psi_raw(vals: np.ndarray, gamma: float) -> np.ndarray:
    return frac(gamma * np.log(vals.astype(np.float64)))

def psi_centered(vals: np.ndarray, gamma: float) -> np.ndarray:
    x = vals.astype(np.float64)
    return frac(gamma * (np.log(x) - np.log(np.log(x))))

def fit_arc_score_model(train_pr: np.ndarray, train_co: np.ndarray, M: int, gamma: float, edges: np.ndarray, alpha: float, psi_fn) -> np.ndarray:
    bins = len(edges) - 1
    P = np.zeros((M, bins), dtype=np.float64)
    C = np.zeros((M, bins), dtype=np.float64)

    def add(vals, H):
        r = (vals % M).astype(int)
        psi = psi_fn(vals, gamma)
        b = np.clip(np.digitize(psi, edges) - 1, 0, bins-1)
        # vectorized accumulation via bincount on flattened indices
        idx = r * bins + b
        bc = np.bincount(idx, minlength=M*bins).astype(np.float64)
        H += bc.reshape(M, bins)

    add(train_pr, P)
    add(train_co, C)

    cop = (np.gcd(np.arange(M), M) == 1)
    P[~cop] = 0.0
    C[~cop] = 0.0

    return np.log((P + alpha) / (C + alpha))

def score_with_table(vals: np.ndarray, M: int, gamma: float, edges: np.ndarray, table: np.ndarray, psi_fn) -> np.ndarray:
    bins = len(edges) - 1
    r = (vals % M).astype(int)
    psi = psi_fn(vals, gamma)
    b = np.clip(np.digitize(psi, edges) - 1, 0, bins-1)
    return table[r, b].astype(np.float64)

def eval_model(table, test_pr, test_co, M, gamma, edges, psi_fn):
    sp = score_with_table(test_pr, M, gamma, edges, table, psi_fn)
    sc = score_with_table(test_co, M, gamma, edges, table, psi_fn)
    scores = np.concatenate([sp, sc])
    labels = np.concatenate([np.ones_like(sp, dtype=np.int32), np.zeros_like(sc, dtype=np.int32)])
    auc = auc_roc(scores, labels)

    # retention curve
    def keep_stats(f_keep):
        thr = np.quantile(scores, 1.0 - f_keep)
        return float(thr), float((sp >= thr).mean()), float((sc >= thr).mean())

    curve = [(f,)+keep_stats(f) for f in [0.5, 0.25, 0.1, 0.05, 0.02, 0.01]]
    return auc, curve, sp, sc

# --- Subtask 14B: replace log n with log n - log log n; re-test arc model and re-opt gamma (coarse) ---
M = 2310
Nmax = 600_000
train_low, train_high = M+1, 400_000
test_low, test_high = 400_001, 600_000

bins_psi = 60
edges = np.linspace(0.0, 1.0, bins_psi+1)
alpha = 0.5

rng = np.random.default_rng(2027)
is_prime = sieve_primes(Nmax)
all_primes = np.nonzero(is_prime)[0]
all_primes = all_primes[all_primes >= 2]

# sample sizes (try larger than previous but bounded by speed)
n_train = 60_000
n_test = 25_000

train_pr = sample_primes_in_range(all_primes, train_low, train_high, n_train, rng)
train_co = sample_wheel_composites_in_range(train_low, train_high, M, is_prime, n_train, rng)
test_pr  = sample_primes_in_range(all_primes, test_low, test_high, n_test, rng)
test_co  = sample_wheel_composites_in_range(test_low, test_high, M, is_prime, n_test, rng)

# Compare raw vs centered at fixed gamma=0.292
gamma0 = 0.292

tab_raw = fit_arc_score_model(train_pr, train_co, M, gamma0, edges, alpha, psi_raw)
auc_raw, curve_raw, sp_raw, sc_raw = eval_model(tab_raw, test_pr, test_co, M, gamma0, edges, psi_raw)

tab_ctr = fit_arc_score_model(train_pr, train_co, M, gamma0, edges, alpha, psi_centered)
auc_ctr, curve_ctr, sp_ctr, sc_ctr = eval_model(tab_ctr, test_pr, test_co, M, gamma0, edges, psi_centered)

# Coarse gamma optimization for centered psi'
gamma_grid = np.round(np.linspace(0.22, 0.38, 17), 6)
aucs = []
for g in gamma_grid:
    tab = fit_arc_score_model(train_pr, train_co, M, float(g), edges, alpha, psi_centered)
    auc_g, _, _, _ = eval_model(tab, test_pr, test_co, M, float(g), edges, psi_centered)
    aucs.append(auc_g)
aucs = np.array(aucs, dtype=np.float64)
best_idx = int(np.argmax(aucs))
gamma_best = float(gamma_grid[best_idx])
auc_best = float(aucs[best_idx])

tab_best = fit_arc_score_model(train_pr, train_co, M, gamma_best, edges, alpha, psi_centered)
auc_best2, curve_best, sp_best, sc_best = eval_model(tab_best, test_pr, test_co, M, gamma_best, edges, psi_centered)

# --- plots ---
fig = plt.figure(figsize=(12, 5))
ax1 = plt.subplot(1,2,1)
ax1.hist(sp_raw, bins=70, alpha=0.7, density=True, label=f"primes raw (AUC={auc_raw:.3f})")
ax1.hist(sc_raw, bins=70, alpha=0.4, density=True, label="composites raw")
ax1.set_title("Score distributions (raw ψ)")
ax1.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
ax1.legend(frameon=False)

ax2 = plt.subplot(1,2,2)
ax2.hist(sp_ctr, bins=70, alpha=0.7, density=True, label=f"primes centered (AUC={auc_ctr:.3f})")
ax2.hist(sc_ctr, bins=70, alpha=0.4, density=True, label="composites centered")
ax2.set_title("Score distributions (ψ' = frac(γ(log n - log log n)))")
ax2.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
ax2.legend(frameon=False)

plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

fig = plt.figure(figsize=(12, 4))
plt.plot(gamma_grid, aucs, marker='o', linestyle='-')
plt.axvline(gamma0, linestyle='--', linewidth=1, label="0.292")
plt.title("Coarse gamma sweep for centered ψ' (AUC on test)")
plt.xlabel("gamma")
plt.ylabel("AUC")
plt.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
plt.legend(frameon=False)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

# retention curve plot for best centered model
fig = plt.figure(figsize=(7, 5))
f_keep = np.array([c[0] for c in curve_best])
pr_keep = np.array([c[2] for c in curve_best])
co_keep = np.array([c[3] for c in curve_best])
plt.plot(f_keep, pr_keep, marker='o', linestyle='-', label="prime kept")
plt.plot(f_keep, co_keep, marker='o', linestyle='-', label="composite kept")
plt.xscale("log")
plt.title(f"Retention (centered ψ') at gamma={gamma_best:.3f}, AUC={auc_best2:.3f}")
plt.xlabel("fraction of candidates kept")
plt.ylabel("kept fraction within class")
plt.grid(True, alpha=0.2)
plt.legend(frameon=False)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

### Assistant (2026-02-07 19:38:32)

### Subtask 14 (executed): make \(\psi\) scale-adaptive and see if the “arc rule” starts behaving like an actual sieve

You asked for the next step. I tried the most prime-theory-flavored correction first:

\[
\psi'(n)=\mathrm{frac}\Big(\gamma\big(\log n-\log\log n\big)\Big)
\]

Then I reran the **per-residue arc likelihood** model (same architecture as Subtask 13) with:
- Train: \(2311 \to 400{,}000\)
- Test: \(400{,}001 \to 600{,}000\)
- \(M=2310\), 60 \(\psi\)-bins, smoothing \(\alpha=0.5\)

Because if a coordinate change can fix the generalization failure, it should show up here.

---

## Results

### 1) Raw \(\psi=\mathrm{frac}(\gamma\log n)\) at \(\gamma=0.292\)
- **AUC = 0.5035** (still basically random)

### 2) Centered \(\psi'=\mathrm{frac}(\gamma(\log n-\log\log n))\) at \(\gamma=0.292\)
- **AUC = 0.5001** (even more random)

So the simple “PNT-style centering” did **not** magically stabilize the arcs.

---

## Coarse re-optimization of \(\gamma\) for \(\psi'\)
Since \(\gamma=0.292\) came from the old coordinate, I swept \(\gamma\in[0.22,0.38]\) (17 points).

- Best in this sweep: **\(\gamma=0.360\)**
- **Best AUC = 0.5058**

That is technically above 0.5, and practically still “coin flip with delusions of grandeur.”

---

## Does it at least filter better?
Using the best centered model (\(\gamma=0.360\)), I measured retention when keeping only the top-scoring fraction of candidates:

At **keep 25%** of candidates:
- primes kept: **0.260**
- composites kept: **0.245**

At **keep 10%**:
- primes kept: **0.251**
- composites kept: **0.237**

So there’s a *tiny* tilt in the right direction (primes kept slightly more), but it’s nowhere near “sieve power.” It’s more like “a weak preference that doesn’t scale into a law.”

Also note: these retention curves are quantile-based over the test mixture, so the thresholding can get weird when the score distribution has heavy ties (you’ll notice repeated thresholds like \(\log 3\)).

---

# What we learned
- The “residue-conditioned arc structure” is real (JS divergences), but it **still doesn’t translate into a stable predictive rule** even after a reasonable scale correction.
- If there’s an equation hiding here, it’s likely not a **static** per-residue arc-set in \(\psi\).
- More likely: the “arc preference” is **nonstationary** and depends on additional state, e.g.:
  - local scale window,
  - finer modulus (like extending beyond 2310),
  - or interactions between multiple phase coordinates (not just one \(\psi\)).

---

## Next subtask (the one that’s actually worth doing now)
**Subtask 15: add a second phase coordinate and learn arcs in 2D phase-space**
Instead of trying to squeeze prime structure into one circle, use a torus:

\[
\psi_1=\mathrm{frac}(\gamma_1\log n),\quad
\psi_2=\mathrm{frac}(\gamma_2\log n)
\]
(or use \(\log n - \log\log n\) for one of them)

Then learn per-residue **2D** density ratios (2D bins) and test generalization.

Why this is the sensible move:
- your earlier “4D polar” instinct basically demands *at least* two independent angles
- the 1D arc-set is too lossy, and it keeps collapsing into non-predictive distribution shifts

If you keep forcing a 1D phase to carry prime structure, you’ll keep getting AUCs that hover around “maybe the random number generator is tired.”
